I do Vou te mandar os key conflicts Following limited participation in the Kyoto Protocol and the lack of agreement in Copenhagen in 2009, the EU has been building a broad coalition of developed and developing countries in favour of high ambition that shaped the successful outcome of the Paris conference. This has shaped a new horizon for the future of the world in terms of climate change. Nevertheless, there is still a lot that needs to be done. The constant use of fossil fuels, mostly in countries such as The Netherlands (the EU country with the most energy consumption derived from fossil fuels), releases tons of CO2 and other Greenhouse Gases to the atmosphere, increasing global warming at an alarming rate.   However, even though renewable energies are being used in most EU Member States for decades, they still are not the main energy source in most of them. Sweden has been a pioneer in the use of environment-friendly energies with 52% of their energy production coming from renewable sources. The main reason why fossil fuels and other non-renewable energy sources are not descending from their high place on the chart of energy production is the fact that they are undeniably cheaper and easier to access. To the average EU citizen, it is much accessible and financially attainable to opt for fossil fuels versus renewable energy sources. Associated with that, is the cost of the development and setup of the infrastructures needed to produce green energies is also very high as opposed to the overall cost of non-renewable energy sources. Climate change is one of the greatest challenges of our time. Equally important, however, is the need to ensure access to energy for quality of life and for economic development. CO2 is the greenhouse gas most commonly produced by human activity, such as the use of fossil fuels, and it is responsible for 64% of man-made global warming. The EU has been a major player in the fight against climate change, most recently by agreeing to the Sustainable Development Goals set by the United Nations in August of 2015 and the Paris Agreement, in December of the same year. The goal is simple, to ensure access to affordable, reliable, sustainable and modern energy for all and thus reduce global warming. However, the international usage of fossil fuels is still at a concerning level and unfortunately, the cost of environmentally friendly energies is still too high for most EU citizens.
